                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome of the seagrass Zostera marina reveals angiosperm adaptation to the sea"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2016 530:7590"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-01-27"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Whole-genome sequencing of the seagrass Zostera, representing the first marine angiosperm genome to be fully sequenced, provides insight into the evolutionary changes associated with a transition to a marine environment in this angiosperm lineage. The seagrass Zostera marina, or eelgrass, is widely distributed throughout the Northern Hemisphere. It is therefore of considerable ecological importance but &#8212; as with other seagrasses &#8212; its coastal habitats are among the world&#39;s most threatened ecosystems. Jeanine Olsen and colleagues report the whole-genome sequence of Zostera. Their analyses provide insights into the evolutionary changes associated with the &#39;back to the sea&#39; reverse evolutionary trajectory that has occurred in this angiosperm lineage, including the loss of the entire repertoire of stomatal genes, and the presence of sulfated cell-wall polysaccharides that are more macro-algal-like than plant-like. Seagrasses colonized the sea1 on at least three independent occasions to form the basis of one of the most productive and widespread coastal ecosystems on the planet2. Here we report the genome of Zostera marina (L.), the first, to our knowledge, marine angiosperm to be fully sequenced. This reveals unique insights into the genomic losses and gains involved in achieving the structural and physiological adaptations required for its marine lifestyle, arguably the most severe habitat shift ever accomplished by flowering plants. Key angiosperm innovations that were lost include the entire repertoire of stomatal genes3, genes involved in the synthesis of terpenoids and ethylene signalling, and genes for ultraviolet protection and phytochromes for far-red sensing. Seagrasses have also regained functions enabling them to adjust to full salinity. Their cell walls contain all of the polysaccharides typical of land plants, but also contain polyanionic, low-methylated pectins and sulfated galactans, a feature shared with the cell walls of all macroalgae4 and that is important for ion homoeostasis, nutrient uptake and O2/CO2 exchange through leaf epidermal cells. The Z. marina genome resource will markedly advance a wide range of functional ecological studies from adaptation of marine ecosystems under climate warming5,6, to unravelling the mechanisms of osmoregulation under high salinities that may further inform our understanding of the evolution of salt tolerance in crop plants7."/>

                    <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">The genome of the seagrass <i>Zostera marina</i> reveals angiosperm adaptation to the sea</h1>

Olsen<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Groningen Institute of Evolutionary Life Sciences Pearson</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff17">17</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Marine Ecology and Evolution, Centre for Marine Sciences (CCMAR), University of Algarve" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.7157.4, 0000 0000 9693 350X, Marine Ecology and Evolution, Centre for Marine Sciences (CCMAR), University of Algarve, Faro, 8005-139, Portugal" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Gabriele-Procaccini" data-author-popup="auth-Gabriele-Procaccini">Gabriele Procaccini</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Stazione Zoologica Anton Dohrn" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.6401.3, 0000 0004 1758 0806, Stazione Zoologica Anton Dohrn, Villa Comunale, Naples, 80121, Italy" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na1">na1</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Carlos_M_-Duarte" data-author-popup="auth-Carlos_M_-Duarte">Carlos M. Duarte</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff18">18</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="King Abdullah University of Science and Technology (KAUST), Red Sea Research Center (RSRC)" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.45672.32, 0000 0001 1926 5090, King Abdullah University of Science and Technology (KAUST), Red Sea Research Center (RSRC), Thuwal, 23955-6900, Saudi Arabia" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jeremy-Schmutz" data-author-popup="auth-Jeremy-Schmutz">Jeremy Schmutz</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff11">11</a>,<a href="#Aff13">13</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.451309.a, 0000 0004 0449 479X, Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute, 2800 Mitchell Dr., #100, Walnut Creek, 94598, California, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="HudsonAlpha Institute for Biotechnology" /><meta itemprop="address" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Thorsten_B__H_-Reusch" data-author-popup="auth-Thorsten_B__H_-Reusch">Thorsten B. H. Reusch</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a>,<a href="#Aff19">19</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="GEOMAR Helmholtz Centre for Ocean Research-Kiel, Evolutionary Ecology" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.15649.3f, 0000 0000 9056 9663, GEOMAR Helmholtz Centre for Ocean Research-Kiel, Evolutionary Ecology, Düsternbrooker Weg 20, Kiel, D-24105, Germany" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Kiel, Faculty of Mathematics and Natural Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.9764.c, 0000 0001 2153 9986, University of Kiel, Faculty of Mathematics and Natural Sciences, Christian-Albrechts-Platz 4, Kiel, 24118, Germany" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na1">na1</a></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Yves-Van_de_Peer" data-author-popup="auth-Yves-Van_de_Peer" data-corresp-id="c2">Yves Van de Peer<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a>,<a href="#Aff20">20</a>,<a href="#Aff21">21</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Ghent University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5342.0, 0000 0001 2069 7798, Department of Plant Systems Biology, VIB and Department of Plant Biotechnology and Bioinformatics, Ghent University, Technologiepark 927, Ghent, B-9052, Belgium" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Genomics Research Institute, University of Pretoria" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.49697.35, 0000 0001 2107 2298, Genomics Research Institute, University of Pretoria, Hatfield Campus, Pretoria, 0028, South Africa" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Bioinformatics Institute Ghent, Ghent University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5342.0, 0000 0001 2069 7798, Bioinformatics Institute Ghent, Ghent University, Ghent B-9000, Belgium" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na1">na1</a></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p>Seagrasses colonized the sea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Les, D. H., Cleland, M. A. &amp; Waycott, M. Phylogenetic studies in Alismatidae, II: evolution of marine angiosperms (seagrasses) and hydrophily. Syst. Bot. 22, 443–463 (1997)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1119">1</a></sup> on at least three independent occasions to form the basis of one of the most productive and widespread coastal ecosystems on the planet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Larkum, W. D., Orth, R. J. &amp; Duarte, C. M. Seagrasses: Biology, Ecology and Conservation (Springer, Dordrecht, Netherlands, 2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1123">2</a></sup>. Here we report the genome of <i>Zostera marina</i> (L.), the first, to our knowledge, marine angiosperm to be fully sequenced. This reveals unique insights into the genomic losses and gains involved in achieving the structural and physiological adaptations required for its marine lifestyle, arguably the most severe habitat shift ever accomplished by flowering plants. Key angiosperm innovations that were lost include the entire repertoire of stomatal genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Berry, J. A., Beerling, D. J. &amp; Franks, P. J. Stomata: key players in the earth system, past and present. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol . 13, 232–239 (2010)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1130">3</a></sup>, genes involved in the synthesis of terpenoids and ethylene signalling, and genes for ultraviolet protection and phytochromes for far-red sensing. Seagrasses have also regained functions enabling them to adjust to full salinity. Their cell walls contain all of the polysaccharides typical of land plants, but also contain polyanionic, low-methylated pectins and sulfated galactans, a feature shared with the cell walls of all macroalgae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Aquino, R. S., Landeira-Fernandez, A. M., Valente, A. P., Andrade, L. R. &amp; Mourao, P. A. S. Occurrence of sulfated galactans in marine angiosperms: evolutionary implications. Glycobiology 15, 11–20 (2005)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1134">4</a></sup> and that is important for ion homoeostasis, nutrient uptake and O<sub>2</sub>/CO<sub>2</sub> exchange through leaf epidermal cells. The <i>Z. marina</i> genome resource will markedly advance a wide range of functional ecological studies from adaptation of marine ecosystems under climate warming<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Franssen, S. U. et al. Transcriptomic resilience to global warming in the seagrass Zostera marina, a marine foundation species. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 19276–19281 (2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1146">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Mazzuca, S. et al. Establishing research strategies, methodologies and technologies to link genomics and proteomics to seagrass productivity, community metabolism, and ecosystem carbon fluxes. Front. Plant Sci. 4, 1–19 (2013)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1149">6</a></sup>, to unravelling the mechanisms of osmoregulation under high salinities that may further inform our understanding of the evolution of salt tolerance in crop plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Duarte, C. M. et al. Will the oceans help feed humanity? Bioscience 59, 967–976 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1153">7</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Seagrasses are a polyphyletic assemblage of basal monocots belonging to four families in the Alismatales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Les, D. H., Cleland, M. A. &amp; Waycott, M. Phylogenetic studies in Alismatidae, II: evolution of marine angiosperms (seagrasses) and hydrophily. Syst. Bot. 22, 443–463 (1997)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1189">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Larkum, W. D., Orth, R. J. &amp; Duarte, C. M. Seagrasses: Biology, Ecology and Conservation (Springer, Dordrecht, Netherlands, 2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1192">2</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 1.1 and Supplementary Fig. 1.1</a>). As a functional group, they provide the foundation of highly productive ecosystems present along the coasts of all continents except Antarctica, where they rival tropical rain forests and coral reefs in ecosystem services<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Costanza, R. et al. The value of the world’s ecosystem services and natural capital. Nature 387, 253–260 (1997)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1199">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Fourqurean, J. W. et al. Seagrass ecosystems as a globally significant carbon stock. Nature Geosci. 5, 505–509 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1202">9</a></sup>. In colonizing sedimentary shorelines of the world’s ocean, seagrasses found a vast new habitat free of terrestrial competitors and insect pests but had to adapt to cope with new structural and physiological challenges related to full marine conditions.</p><p><i>Zostera marina</i> (Zosteraceae), or eelgrass (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), is the most widespread species throughout the temperate northern hemisphere of the Pacific and Atlantic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Green, E. P. &amp; Short, F. T. World Atlas of Seagrasses (University of California Press, Berkeley, CA, USA, 2003)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1214">10</a></sup>. A clone of <i>Z. marina</i> was sequenced from the Archipelago Sea, southwest Finland, using a combination of fosmid-ends and whole-genome shotgun (WGS) approaches (Methods, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 2</a>). The 202.3 Mb <i>Z. marina</i> genome encodes 20,450 protein-coding genes, 86.6% of which (17,511 genes, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 3.1</a>) are supported by transcriptome data from leaves, roots and flowers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Notes 3.2–3.3 and Supplementary Data 1–3</a>). Genes are located in numerous gene-dense islands separated by stretches of repeat elements accounting for 63% of the non-gapped assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 3.1</a>) as compared to only 13% in the only other sequenced alismatid, the freshwater duckweek, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> (Alismatales, Araceae)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nature Commun . 5, 1–13 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1247">11</a></sup>. Gypsy-type (32%) and Copia-type (20%) transposable elements contribute to most of the repetitive DNA. Sequence divergence analysis suggests that the genome retains copies from two distinct periods of invasion by Copia elements, but only one period for Gypsy elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3a–c</a>). Genes gained by <i>Z. marina</i> (‘accessory’) are located closer to transposable elements than to conserved (‘single copy’) genes (Fisher’s exact test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001) indicating that transposable elements may have played a role in genic adaptation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Zostera marina and phylogenetic tree showing gene family expansion/contraction analysis compared with 13 representatives of the Viridiplantae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <i>Zostera marina</i> and phylogenetic tree showing gene family expansion/contraction analysis compared with 13 representatives of the Viridiplantae.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="324" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Gains and losses are indicated along branches and nodes. The number of gene families, orphans (single-copy gene families) and number of predicted genes is indicated next to each species. Background colours (top to bottom) are Alismatales, other monocots, dicots, mosses/algae <b>b</b>, Typical <i>Zostera marina</i> meadow, Archipelago Sea, southwest Finland (photo by C.B.).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM16">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified 36 conserved microRNAs with high confidence and their predicted targets (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 3.4, Supplementary Data 4 and 5</a>). A novel variant of miR528 (not present in <i>Spirodela</i>) was found to be the only member of this miRNA family, and demonstrates that this conserved miRNA is the only one ancestral to the entire monocot lineage. Most likely, <i>Z. marina</i> did not take part in the subsequent birth of miRNAs that are common to several other monocots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chavez Montes, R. A. et al. Sample sequencing of vascular plants demonstrates widespread conservation and divergence of microRNAs. Nature Commun . 5, 1–15 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1309">12</a></sup>; nor did it experience or retain traces of prominent miRNA duplications.</p><p>Analysis of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>S</sub>) age distributions indicates that <i>Z. marina</i> carries the remnants of an independent, ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 4.1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vanneste, K., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Tangled up in two: a burst of genome duplications at the end of the Cretaceous and the consequences for plant evolution. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. B Biol. Sci . 369, 20130353 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1329">13</a></sup>. Duplicated segments account for ~9% of the <i>Z. marina</i> genome, probably an underestimate due to the fragmented nature of the assembly. <i>Zostera</i> and <i>Spirodela</i> diverged somewhere between 135 and 107 million years ago (Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nauheimer, L., Metzler, D. &amp; Renner, S. S. Global history of the ancient monocot family Araceae inferred with models accounting for past continental positions and previous ranges based on fossils. New Phytol. 195, 938–950 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1343">14</a></sup> and phylogenomic dating<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vanneste, K., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Tangled up in two: a burst of genome duplications at the end of the Cretaceous and the consequences for plant evolution. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. B Biol. Sci . 369, 20130353 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1347">13</a></sup> of the <i>Z. marina</i> WGD suggests that it occurred 72–64 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), thus independently from the two WGDs reported for <i>S. polyrhiza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nature Commun . 5, 1–13 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1360">11</a></sup>. This timeframe coincides with the initial diversification of a freshwater clade that includes three of the four families of seagrasses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 1.1</a>) and with the Cretaceous–Palaeogene (K–Pg) extinction event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>), which provided new ecological opportunities and may have triggered seagrass adaptive radiations.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="713" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <i>K</i><sub>S</sub>-based age distribution of the whole <i>Z. marina</i> paranome. The <i>x</i> axis shows the synonymous distance until a <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> cut-off of 2, in bins of 0.04, containing the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values that were used for mixture modelling (excluding those with a <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> ≤ 0.1). The component of the Gaussian mixture model plotted in red (as identified by EMMIX) corresponds to a WGD feature based on the SiZer analysis (other components are shown in black). The transition from the blue to the red at a <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> of ~0.8 in the SiZer panel (below) indicates a change in the distribution and therefore provides evidence for an ancient WGD (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 4.1, Supplementary Fig. 4.1</a>). <b>b</b>, Absolute age distribution obtained by phylogenomic dating of <i>Z. marina</i> paralogues. The solid black line represents the kernel density estimate (KDE) of the dated paralogues and the vertical dashed black line represents its peak, used as the consensus WGD age estimate, at 67 Mya. Grey lines represent the density estimates from 2,500 bootstrap replicates and the vertical black dotted lines represent the corresponding 90% confidence interval for the WGD age estimate, 64–72 Mya. The original raw distribution of dated paralogues is indicated by the circles. The <i>y</i> axis represents the percentage of gene pairs. <b>c</b>, Pruned phylogenetic tree with indication of WGD events (boxes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1427">29</a></sup>. The Cretaceous–Palaeogene (K–Pg) boundary is indicated by an arrow.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM17">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We mapped signatures of loss and gain of gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 4.2</a>) onto a phylogenetic tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). We also mapped losses and gains of Pfam domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Fig. 4.4, Supplementary Data 6</a>). While many genes are shared between <i>Zostera</i> and <i>Spriodela</i>, clearly some losses and gains are unique to <i>Zostera</i> in relation to its marine environment, the alismatid lineage having set the stage for the subsequent freshwater–marine transition. Those unique to <i>Z. marina</i> include the absence of all the genes involved in stomatal differentiation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab1">Extended Data Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 5.1</a>) and the disappearance of genes comprising entire pathways encoding volatiles synthesis and sensing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 6.1</a>), such as those for ethylene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Golicz, A. A. et al. Genome-wide survejy of the seagrass Zostera muelleri suggests modification of the ethylene signalling network. J. Exp. Bot. (2015)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1484">15</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab2">Extended Data Table 2</a>). Terpenoid genes are also drastically reduced to two (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>), as compared with four in <i>Spirodela</i>, 50 in <i>Oryza</i> and &gt; 100 in <i>Eucalyptus</i>, thus precluding synthesis of secondary volatile terpenes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Fig. 6.2</a>). Only aromatic acid decarboxylases (AAAD) genes were expanded (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Fig. 6.3</a>) and these form a clade distinct from <i>Spirodela.</i> The loss of volatiles is also consistent with the loss of stomata, through which they are emitted for airborne communication and plant defence. The repertoire of defence-related genes such as the six groups of NBS_LRR resistance genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 6.2</a>) is also reduced to 44 (89 in <i>Spirodela</i> and 100–300 in other plants), which may be linked to a lower probability of infection of <i>Z. marina</i> due to the absence of stomata, which are a main entry point for pests and pathogens in terrestrial plants.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Reconstruction of metabolic (or gene) pathways involved in the production of stomata, ethylene, terpene and pollen in Z. marina."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Reconstruction of metabolic (or gene) pathways involved in the production of stomata, ethylene, terpene and pollen in <i>Z. marina</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="461" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Stomata differentiation from meristemoid mother cells (MMC) to guard mother cell (GMC) to guard cells. <b>b</b>, Ethylene synthesis and signalling up to <i>EIN2</i> have disappeared; <i>EIN3</i> and its downstream targets remain. <b>c</b>, Terpenoid biosynthesis in which the pathways producing volatiles are absent but those essential for primary metabolism remain. MVA, mevalonate; MEP, plastidic methylerythritol phosphate; IPP, isopentenyl pyrophosphate; DMAPP, dimethylallyl pyrophosphate; FPP, farnesyl pyrophosphate; GPP, geranyl diphosphate; GGPP, geranylgeranyl pyrophosphate; CPP, copalyl pyrophosphate; GA, gibberellic acid; PP, diphosphate; ABA, abscisic acid. <b>d</b>, Sporopollenin biosynthesis genes; regulatory genes in the nucleus control downstream processes (arrows) in response to signalling coming from external stimuli through receptors on the plasma membrane. All panels: genes in red are absent; blue are present; the grey line represents the plasma membrane. See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab1">Extended Data Tables 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab2">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab3">3</a>.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM18">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Land and aquatic floating plants (Embryophyta) are often exposed to intense ultraviolet (UV) radiation and have developed light sensing protein receptors with protective and signalling functions. In contrast, <i>Z. marina</i> inhabits a light-attenuated, submarine environment where it must cope with shifted spectral composition, characterized by low penetration of UV-B, red and far-red wavelengths<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kirk, J. T. O. in Light and Photosynthesis in Aquatic Ecosystems (Cambridge Univ. Press, 2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1589">16</a></sup>. Accordingly, <i>Z. marina</i> has lost ultraviolet-resistance (UVR8) genes associated with sensing and responding to UV damage (<i>Spirodela</i> has not), as well as phytochromes associated with red/far-red receptors (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 7</a>). Whereas photosystems (PSI and PSII) are similar to those of other plants including <i>Spirodela</i>, members of the light-harvesting complex B (LHCB) family are expanded in number, possibly in combination with non-photochemical quenching (NPQ), thereby enhancing performance at low light (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 4</a>).</p><p>Seagrasses typically experience full marine seawater (35 g kg<sup>−1</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Touchette, B. W. Seagrass-salinity interactions: physiological mechanisms used by submersed marine angiosperms for a life at sea. J. Exp. Mar. Biol. Ecol. 350, 194–215 (2007)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1614">17</a></sup>, whereas land plants obtain water with low osmolality (0–2 g kg<sup>−1</sup>) via the rhizosphere and aquatic plants experience fresh (0–5 g kg<sup>−1</sup>) to brackish (0.5–20 g kg<sup>−1</sup>) conditions. Although <i>Z. marina</i> displays a typical repertoire of Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> antiporters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 8, Supplementary Table 8.1</a>), one of six H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (AHA) genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 8.2, Supplementary Data 7</a>) is strongly expressed in vegetative tissue and encodes a salt-tolerant H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase. Furthermore, <i>Z. marina</i> possesses three AHA genes (along with <i>Spirodela</i>) in a cluster unique to alismatids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Fig. 8.1</a>).</p><p>Uniquely, <i>Z. marina</i> has re-evolved new combinations of structural traits related to the cell wall. Synthesis of cutin-cuticular waxes to the outside of the leaf epidermis and suberin–lignin near the plasma membrane (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 9, Supplementary Table 9.1</a>) surround a cell wall matrix of (hemi)celluloses, low-methylated pectin (zosterin) and macroalgal-like sulfated polysaccharides<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Popper, Z. A. et al. Evolution and diversity of plant cell walls: from algae to flowering plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 62, 567–590 (2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1661">18</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 10</a>). The reduction in carbohydrate-related genes that modify the fine structure of cell wall hemicelluloses and pectins in <i>Z. marina</i> is not due to loss of pathways, but rather to the large variation within these CAZyme gene families in plants. Available genomes (including <i>Spirodela</i>) lack carbohydrate sulfotransferases and sulfatases, suggesting that land plants have lost these genes as a key adaptation to terrestrial as well as freshwater conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Michel, G., Tonon, T., Scornet, D., Cock, J. M. &amp; Kloareg, B. The cell wall polysaccharide metabolism of the brown alga Ectocarpus siliculosus: insights into the evolution of extracellular matrix polysaccharides in eukaryotes. New Phytol. 188, 82–97 (2010)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1675">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Collen, J. et al. Genome structure and metabolic features in the red seaweed Chondrus crispus shed light on evolution of the Archaeplastida. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 5247–5252 (2013)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1678">20</a></sup>. In contrast, <i>Z. marina</i> has regained the ability to produce sulfated polysaccharides with an expansion of aryl sulfotransferases (12 genes) homologous to aryl sulfotransferases from land plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 10</a>). Sulfation facilitates water and ion retention in the cell wall to cope with desiccation and osmotic stress at low tide and, likewise, low methylation of zosterin correlates with the expanded pectin carbohydrate esterase 8 (CE8) family, increasing the polyanionic character of the cell wall matrix. We speculate that several aryl sulfotransferases have evolved because carbohydrate sulfatases have been shown to be active on artificial aryl compounds such as methylumbelliferyl-sulfate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Hanson, S. R., Best, M. D. &amp; Wong, C. H. Sulfatases: structure, mechanism, biological activity, inhibition, and synthetic utility. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. 43, 5736–5763 (2004)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1688">21</a></sup>. Osmotic equilibrium is further achieved in <i>Z. marina</i> by organic osmolytes (mainly sucrose, trehalose and proline) in combination with a small cytoplasm:vacuole volume ratio (10%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Larkum, A. W. D., Drew, E. A. &amp; Ralph, P. J. in Seagrasses: Biology, Ecology and Conservation (eds Larkum, A. W. D., Orth, R. J. &amp; Duarte, C. M. ) 323–345 (Springer, Dordrecht, Netherlands, 2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1696">22</a></sup>. Given that up to 90% of fixed carbon is stored as sucrose in the rhizomes, sucrose synthase (SuSy) and transport (SUT) genes are expanded while those for starch metabolism are greatly reduced, as expected in ‘marine sugarcane’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 7.2, Supplementary Data 8</a>).</p><p>The repertoire of redox and other stress-resistance genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 8</a>) is typical for angiosperms with the exception of catalase (CAT), which is reduced to a single copy in <i>Z. marina</i> (two in <i>Spirodela</i>). Late embryogenesis abundant (LEA) and dehydrins are clearly under-represented in both <i>Zostera</i> and <i>Spriodela</i> relative to other genomes. In contrast, <i>Zostera</i> possesses an unusual complement of metallothioneins. Aside from their role as chelators, metallothioneins may be involved in stress resistance; one of these, MT2L, is among the most highly constitutively expressed genes in <i>Z. marina</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 8.2</a>).</p><p>Sexual reproduction of <i>Z. marina</i> takes place underwater, involving completely submerged male and female flowers, and a unique exine-less, filiform pollen that winds around the bifurcate stigmas in a purely abiotic pollination process<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="De Cock, A. W. Flowering, pollinations and fruiting in Zostera marina L. Aquat. Bot. 9, 201–220 (1980)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1740">23</a></sup>. Note that freshwater alismatids (and also <i>Spirodela</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Furness, C. A. in Early Events in Monocot Evolution (eds Wilkin, P. &amp; Mayo, S. J. ) 1–22 (Cambridge Univ. Press, 2013)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1747">24</a></sup> possess pollen with an exine layer. Exine-less pollen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kuo, J. &amp; den Hartog, C. in Seagrasses: Biology, Ecology and Conservation (eds Larkum, A. W. D., Orth, R. J. &amp; Duarte, C. M. ) 51–87 (Springer, 2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1751">25</a></sup> is characteristic of all seagrasses except <i>Enhalus acoroides</i> (which is surface pollinated). Ten genes specifically involved in biosynthesis and modification of the pollen exine coat are missing; all other genes involved in the development of viable pollen remain intact (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab3">Extended Data Table 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 11.1</a>). Finally, MADS-box gene transcription factors are also highly reduced to 50 in <i>Z. marina</i>, which is most likely related to its highly reduced flowers (also a feature of <i>Spirodela)</i> that lack the first two whorls of specialized floral leaves, calyx and corolla (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 11.2, Supplementary Table 11.2</a>).</p><p>An increasing proportion of the world population inhabits the coastal zone. This impinges multiple pressures on ecosystems including seagrass beds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Orth, R. J. et al. A global crisis for seagrass ecosystems. Bioscience 56, 987–996 (2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1782">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Waycott, M. et al. Accelerating loss of seagrasses across the globe threatens coastal ecosystems. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 12377–12381 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1785">27</a></sup>, which in turn compromises the ecosystem services they may provide, including provisioning of harvestable fish and invertebrates, nutrient retention, carbon sequestration and erosion control. In the context of seagrass conservation, elucidating the genomic basis of <i>Z. marina</i>’s complex adaptations to ocean waters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 6</a>) will also inform the development of molecular indicators of their physiological status<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Macreadie, P. I., Schliepl, M. T., Rasheed, M. A., Chartrand, K. M. &amp; Ralph, P. J. Molecular indicators of chronic seagrass stress: a new era in the management of seagrass ecosystems? Ecol. Indic. 38, 279–281 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1795">28</a></sup>, as these unique ecosystems rank, unfortunately, among the most threatened on Earth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Orth, R. J. et al. A global crisis for seagrass ecosystems. Bioscience 56, 987–996 (2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1799">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Waycott, M. et al. Accelerating loss of seagrasses across the globe threatens coastal ecosystems. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 12377–12381 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1802">27</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The experiments were not randomized and the investigators were not blinded to allocation during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plant material and DNA preparation</h3><p>A single genotype/clone of <i>Zostera marina</i> (referred to as the ‘Finnish clone’) was harvested on 26 August 2010 at 2 m depth at Fårö Island (latitude 59° 55.234′ N longitude 21° 47.766′ E) located in the northern Baltic Sea, Finland. Plant material was transported to the lab in seawater, cleaned and further processed. Care was taken to use leaf-meristem tissue harvested from the inner layer of basal shoots to minimize bacterial/diatom contamination. Tissues were immediately frozen in LN<sub>2</sub> and stored at −80 °C for later DNA and RNA extraction. Monoclonality was verified by genotyping 40 ramets of the mega-clone with six highly polymorphic, microsatellite loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Olsen, J. L. et al. Eelgrass Zostera marina populations in northern Norwegian fjords are genetically isolated and diverse. Mar. Ecol. Prog. Ser. 486, 121–132 (2013)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1826">30</a></sup>. There was no evidence for polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kuo, J. &amp; den Hartog, C. in Seagrasses: Biology, Ecology and Conservation (eds Larkum, A. W. D., Orth, R. J. &amp; Duarte, C. M. ) 51–87 (Springer, 2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1830">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="den Hartog, C., Hennen, J., Noten, T. M. P. A. &amp; Van Wijk, R. J. Chromosome numbers of the European seagrasses. Plant Syst. Evol. 156, 55–59 (1987)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1833">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kuo, J. Chromosome numbers of the Australian Zosteraceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 226, 155–163 (2001)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1836">32</a></sup> (<i>Z. marina</i> is 2n = 12) or somatic mutations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Reusch, T. B. H. &amp; Bostrom, C. Widespread genetic mosaicism in the marine angiosperm Zostera marina is correlated with clonal reproduction. Evol. Ecol. 25, 899–913 (2010)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1844">33</a></sup> as assessed by multiple peaks in the microsatellite chromatograms. Tissue was subsequently sent on dry ice to Amplicon Express for HMW DNA extraction using a CTAB isolation method modified by R. Meilan (unpublished) but available from him (rmeilan@purdue.edu), based on the original method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Doyle, J. L. Isolation of plant DNA from fresh tissue. Focus 12, 13–15 (1990)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1848">34</a></sup>. Following QC according to JGI guidelines, the DNA was shipped to JGI for library and sequencing preparation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>One 35-Kb, fosmid library was generated for end sequencing. The fosmid ends were sequenced with standard Sanger sequencing protocols at the HudsonAlpha Institute for a total of 194,303 Sanger reads (0.29× coverage). Illumina libraries (two fragment libraries (6.62 Gb), one 2-Kb JGI mate-pair library (3.57 Gb), one 4-Kb JGI mate-pair library (3.41 Gb) and two 8-Kb JGI mate-pair libraries (11.94 Gb)) were sequenced with Illumina MiSeq/HiSeq genetic analysers at the Department of Energy’s Joint Genome Institute (JGI), using standard protocols. A total of 25.55 Gb of Illumina and 0.14 Gb of Sanger sequence was obtained representing 47.7× genomic coverage. Prior to assembly, all reads were screened against mitochondria, chloroplast, and Illumina controls. Reads composed of &gt; 95% simple sequence repeats were removed. For the Illumina, paired-end libraries (2 × 250), reads &lt;75 bp were discarded, for the 2 × 150 libraries, reads &lt;50 bp were discarded after trimming for adaptor and quality (<i>q</i> &lt; 20). An additional deduplication step was performed on the mate pairs that identified and retained only one copy of each PCR duplicate. A total of 212,101,273 reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 2.1</a>) was assembled using our modified version of Arachne v. 20071016 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Jaffe, D. B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91–96 (2003)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1865">35</a>). Subsequent directed Arachne modules were applied to collapse adjacent heterozygous contigs. The entire assembly was then run through another Arachne process starting at Stage 6 Rebuilder. This produced 15,747 scaffold sequences (30,723 contigs), with a scaffold L50 of 409.5 Kb, 613 scaffolds larger than 100 Kb, and a total genome size of 237.5 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 2.2</a>).</p><p>Scaffolds were screened against bacterial proteins, organelle sequences, GenBank NR (nr_prot) and RefSeq protein databases, and removed if found to be a contaminant. Scaffolds consisting of prokaryotes, chloroplast, mitochondria and unanchored rDNA were removed. We also assembled the chloroplast and partial mitochondrial genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Notes 2.2 and 2.3, Supplementary Fig. 2.1</a>). Additionally, short (&lt;1 Kb) scaffolds or scaffolds containing highly repetitive sequence ( &gt; 95% 24-mers found more than four times in large scaffolds) or alternative haplotypes were also removed. Following repeat analysis and gene prediction, all scaffolds were subjected to a filtering process (based on NCBI nr_prot + NCBI taxonomy database) to eliminate remaining bacterial (and other) contaminants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 2.3</a>).</p><p>Assembly validation was performed using a set of 12 fully sequenced fosmid clones. In 4 of the 12 fosmid clones, full-length alignments were not found due to fragmentation in the region of the fosmid clone. In five of the remaining eight fosmid clones, the alignments were of high quality (&lt;0.05% bp error). The overall base pair error rate (including marked gap bases) in the fosmid clones that aligned to full length was 0.28% (714 discrepant base pairs out of 253,332 bp). <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 2.4</a> shows the individual fosmid clones and their contribution to the overall error rate. Note that two fosmid clones (16248, 16249) contributed nearly 81% of the discrepant bases. This probably occurred in polymorphic regions of the genome where the haplotype in the fosmid did not match the haplotype in the reference. There are several indels of various sizes in the clone and assembly, typical of a region of degraded transposons. Further quality analysis indicated that 90% of the set of eukaryotic core genes (CEGMA) were present and 98% were partially represented, suggesting near completeness of the euchromatin component.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Annotation of repetitive sequences</h3><p>Two complementary approaches were used to identify repetitive DNA sequences in the <i>Z. marina</i> genome. With respect to masking repeats before gene prediction analysis, a <i>de novo</i> repeat identification was carried out with RepeatModeller (v. open-1.0.7; <a href="http://www.RepeatMasker.org">http://www.RepeatMasker.org</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Smit, A. &amp; Hubley, R. in RepeatModeler Open-1.0 (Repeat Masker Website, &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org/&#xA;                    &#xA;                   2010)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1908">36</a></sup> to identify repeat boundaries and build consensus models from which potential over represented, non-transposable element, protein-coding genes were removed. RepeatMasker (v. open-4.0.0, WUBlast) was used in combination with this custom repeat library to mask the assembly and prepare it for gene prediction with EuGene.</p><p>Furthermore, in order to perform a qualitative and quantitative analysis of repeats with greater resolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Maumus, F. &amp; Quesneville, H. Deep investigation of Arabidopsis thaliana junk DNA reveals a continuum between repetitive elements and genomic dark matter. PLoS ONE 9, e94101 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1915">37</a></sup> the genome assembly was processed for <i>de novo</i> repeat detection using the TEdenovo pipeline from the REPET package v. 2.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Flutre, T., Duprat, E., Feuillet, C. &amp; Quesneville, H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS ONE 6, e16526 (2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1921">38</a>); parameters were set to consider repeats with at least five copies. The consensus sequences generated by TEdenovo were then used as probes for whole genome annotation by the TEannot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Quesneville, H. et al. Combined evidence annotation of transposable elements in genome sequences. PLOS Comput. Biol. 1, e22 (2005)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1925">39</a></sup> pipeline from the REPET package v. 2.2. The consensus repeat sequences were classified using Pastec<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hoede, C. et al. PASTEC: an automatic transposable element classification tool. PLoS ONE 9, e91929 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1929">40</a></sup>. Comparing the genomic positions of transposable elements (TE) to those of exons from the set of predicted genes enabled us to identify that 909 gene predictions most likely represent TEs and these were filtered from the gene set. The REPET package v. 2.2 was also used to annotate repetitive elements in the <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> genome assembly with the same parameters as for <i>Z. marina</i>. See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Fig. 3.1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Transcriptome library preparation, sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Leaf, root and flower tissues were separately frozen in liquid nitrogen immediately following harvest from either ambient (field collected) or experimental (mesocosm) conditions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 3.2</a>). Overall, we obtained between nine and 20 million high-quality reads from each of the flower-leaf-root replicate libraries; and for the Finnish clone library, 148.5 million high quality reads were retrieved (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 3.3</a>).</p><p>The <i>de novo</i> assembly protocol was adapted from ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nature Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1963">41</a>. We pooled replicates of each tissue together except for the two leaf tissue libraries, which were kept separate (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 3.4</a>) and performed <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly for each tissue using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nature Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1973">41</a></sup>(v. 2014-07-17) with digital normalization option ON to normalize input read coverage. Frame shift errors and insertion/deletion errors in the assembled transcripts were corrected by FrameDP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gouzy, J., Carrere, S. &amp; Schiex, T. FrameDP: sensitive peptide detection on noisy matured sequences. Bioinformatics 25, 670–671 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1978">42</a></sup>. Because a <i>de novo</i> assembly still generates many spurious transcripts, we used the transcript expression value to remove low quality contigs. We used the RSEM pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 323 (2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1985">43</a></sup> to obtain the contig expression values and removed contigs with FPKM (fragments per kilobase of transcript per million fragments mapped) value &lt;1 and IsoPct (percentage of expression for a given transcript compared with all expression from that Trinity component) &lt; 1. In total, we obtained between 39,000 and 53,000 assembled contigs from each library, and 52,000 contigs from the Finnish clone library (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 3.4</a>). Prior to mapping the genome sequence and the predicted genes, we used the CD-HIT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 1658–1659 (2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d15965e1992">44</a></sup> program (v. 4.6.1) to collapse redundant contigs, which resulted in 79,134 low redundant transcript contigs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Differential gene expression analysis</h3><p>High-quality RNA-seq reads were mapped to the genome assembly v.2.1 by TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2004">45</a></sup>. Differential gene expression analysis was performed by the Cufflink pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2008">45</a></sup> based on the <i>Z. marina</i> v.2.1 gene models by converting the number of aligned reads into FPKM values. Genes with significant expression difference (log<sub>2</sub> &gt; 2) were selected for further investigation by GOstats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Falcon, S. &amp; Gentleman, R. Using GOstats to test gene lists for GO term association. Bioinformatics 23, 257–258 (2007)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2017">46</a></sup> to perform Gene Ontology (GO) term enrichment analysis with <i>P</i> ≤ 0.05 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 3.3, Supplementary Table 3.5</a>)</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">MicroRNA analysis</h3><p>Genomic precursors of known miRNAs were mapped on the <i>Z. marina</i> genome following the procedure described in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Zhang, L. et al. A genome-wide characterization of microRNA genes in maize. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000716 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2038">47</a> for the maize genome. miRNA entries from the miRBase database (release 21, 2014) were aligned to the chromosomes of the <i>Z. marina</i> genome. Up to three mismatches were allowed in the alignment, using SeqMap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Jiang, H. &amp; Wong, W. H. SeqMap: mapping massive amount of oligonucleotides to the genome. Bioinformatics 24, 2395–2396 (2008)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2045">48</a></sup>. In parallel, novel potential DCL1/AGO1-dependent miRNAs were enriched by selecting 5′-U 20–22 nt small RNAs from three different sequenced libraries from <i>Z. marina</i> described in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chavez Montes, R. A. et al. Sample sequencing of vascular plants demonstrates widespread conservation and divergence of microRNAs. Nature Commun . 5, 1–15 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2052">12</a>. A subset of these small RNAs with abundance ≥10 TPM (transcripts per million) was retained and aligned to the genome with no mismatches. From every locus, we extracted two ~200-nt regions surrounding each aligned miRNA or candidate (from −30 to +160 and from −160 to +30 nucleotides relative to the putative miRNA start or end coordinate, respectively). Minimum energy RNA secondary structures were predicted for each region using the RNAfold program of the Vienna RNA 1.8.5 package (<a href="http://www.tbi.univie.ac.at/~ivo/RNA/">http://www.tbi.univie.ac.at/~ivo/RNA/</a>) using default settings.</p><p>In addition, small RNAs from the three sequenced libraries were mapped on these regions, allowing no mismatches, in order to pre-select putative miRNA loci that showed evidence of expression in the three plant tissues analysed. We evaluated RNA structure and small RNA alignment in all the regions based on: (1) dominance of plus-stranded small RNAs; (2) position of the most abundant small RNAs relative to the predicted miRNA coordinates; (3) prevalence of 20–22 nt small RNAs in the predicted miRNA locus; (4) position of the putative miRNA with the stem-loop structure; and (5) absence of oversize (≥3 nt) bulges in the miRNA/miRNA* alignment. After reduction of overlapping loci to a non-redundant set and removal of stem-loop structures with the wrong orientation compared to miRNAs registered in miRBase, we manually inspected the remaining loci to further evaluate them according to the miRNA annotation criteria proposed by ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Meyers, B. C. et al. Criteria for annotation of plant microRNAs. Plant Cell 20, 3186–3190 (2008)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2065">49</a>. Stringency was relaxed when small RNA expression data strongly indicated the presence of miRNA loci that did not meet the whole set of criteria. Novel miRNA precursors overlapping with TEs or other repetitive elements were filtered out.</p><p>Potential miRNA targets were identified <i>in silico</i> using the generic small RNA-transcriptome aligner <span class="u-small-caps">GST</span>Ar from the CleaveLand package (v. 4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Addo-Quaye, C., Miller, W. &amp; Axtell, M. J. CleaveLand: a pipeline for using degradome data to find cleaved small RNA targets. Bioinformatics 25, 130–131 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2078">50</a></sup>. Predicted targets were accepted with an Allen score &lt;4 or a MFE (minimum free energy) ratio ≥ 7.5. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 3.4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Gene prediction</h3><p>Training of the gene prediction programs started with the collection of high quality ESTs. EST information was used, for example, to train the splice predictor SpliceMachine<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Degroeve, S., Saeys, Y., De Baets, B., Rouze, P. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. SpliceMachine: predicting splice sites from high-dimensional local context representations. Bioinformatics 21, 1332–1338 (2005)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2093">51</a></sup>. Detection of conserved splice sites was further investigated by RNA-seq splice junctions (count &gt; 10) to construct a WAM model in EuGene (v. 4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Foissac, S. et al. Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGene as a model platform. Curr. Bioinformatics 3, 87–97 (2008)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2097">52</a></sup>. Coding-potential was modelled with an interpolated Markov Model (IMM) constructed from the BLASTX alignments of proteins from the PLAZA v. 2.5 database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Van Bel, M. et al. Dissecting plant genomes with the PLAZA comparative genomics platform. Plant Physiol. 158, 590–600 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2101">53</a></sup>. An additional protein ‘monocot’ Markov Model was built based on the protein sequences from <i>Brachypodium</i>, maize and sorghum. Starting from EST and protein alignments, a set of 215 gene models was manually constructed and curated using the genome browser GenomeView<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Abeel, T., Van Parys, T., Saeys, Y., Galagan, J. &amp; Van, P. GenomeView: a next-generation genome browser. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e12 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2108">54</a></sup>. The 215 models were then used as a training set for EuGene in order to optimize the different splice site and coding-potential models, as well as the weights for the extrinsic EST and homology evidence. An overall fitness score of 80.1% was achieved, which is high enough to obtain reliable results without overfitting. GeneMark<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Lomsadze, A., Chernoff, Y. O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene prediction in novel fungal genomes using an ab initio algorithm with unsupervised training. Genome Res. 18, 1979–1990 (2008)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2113">55</a></sup> and Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Stanke, M., Tzvetkova, A. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS at EGASP: using EST, protein and genomic alignments for improved gene prediction in the human genome.
