Genome Biol. 7, S11 (2006)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2117">56</a></sup> were separately trained (using the same input data as EuGene) and their predictions were integrated with EuGene using a custom script to evaluate the best gene structure at each locus. All gene models were automatically screened to highlight possible erroneous structures (for example, in-frame stop codons, deviating splice junctions) and manually curated. Transfer-RNA gene models were predicted by tRNAscan-SE (v. 1.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: A program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2121">57</a></sup> and their structures were verified with Infernal (v. 1.1rc1, rfam11 covariant model database)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Burge, S. W. et al. Rfam 11.0: 10 years of RNA families. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, D226–D232 (2013)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2125">58</a></sup>. For each gene, UTRs were assigned by identifying a set of ESTs and RNA-seq assemblies that uniquely overlapped with it. We subsequently selected the longest mapped transcript on either end of the predicted coding sequence and designated the section outside the coding sequence as the UTR. Finally, all genes were uploaded to the ORCAE platform (<a href="http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/orcae">http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/orcae</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Sterck, L., Billiau, K., Abeel, T., Rouzé, P. &amp; Van der Peer, Y. ORCAE: online resource for community annotation of eukaryotes. Nature Methods 9, 1041 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2136">59</a></sup>, enabling all members of the consortium to refine and curate the gene model and assign gene function. A list of protein domains, as well as the derived Gene Ontology (GO) terms and KEGG pathway identifiers were generated using an InterProScan (v. 5.2.45)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Mitchell, A. et al. The InterPro protein families database: the classification resource after 15 years. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D213–D221 (2015)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2141">60</a></sup> analysis and available in ORCAE. More specifically, gene functional descriptions were added either manually by consortium expert scientists or automatically through sequence homology searches. The automated method relies on the EC (Enzyme Commission) number reported by InterProScan to retrieve the enzyme name with BLASTP search against UniProtKB/Swiss-Prot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="The UniProt Consortium. UniProt: a hub for protein information. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D204–D212 (2015)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2145">61</a></sup> to filter out hits that are below 60% identity and 70% query/hit coverage. Although such high stringency on per cent identity and sequence coverage reduced the available number of functional descriptions, it reduced the false-positive prediction rate, as desired here.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Construction of age distributions and WGD analyses</h3><p><i>K</i><sub>S</sub>-based age distributions were constructed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Vanneste, K., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Maere, S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177–190 (2013)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2160">62</a></sup>. In brief, the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values between genes were obtained through maximum likelihood estimation using the <span class="u-small-caps">CODEML</span> program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Goldman, N. &amp; Yang, Z. A codon-based model of nucleotide substitution for protein-coding DNA sequences. Mol. Biol. Evol. 11, 725–736 (1994)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2171">63</a></sup> of the PAML package (v. 4.4c)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: Phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2176">64</a></sup>. Gene families for which <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates between members did not exceed a value of 5 were subdivided into subfamilies. For each duplicated gene in the resulting phylogenetic gene tree, obtained by PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307–321 (2010)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2184">65</a></sup>, all <i>m K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates between the two child clades were added to the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution with a weight 1/<i>m</i> (where <i>m</i> is the number of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for a duplication event), so that the weights of all <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for a single duplication event summed to one. Mixture modelling was used to confirm a WGD signature in the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Fig. 4.1</a>), for which all duplicates with <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values ≤0.1 were excluded to avoid the incorporation of allelic and/or splice variants, while all duplicates with <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values &gt; 2.0 were removed because <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> saturation and stochasticity can mislead mixture modelling above this range<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Vanneste, K., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Maere, S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177–190 (2013)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2235">62</a></sup>. For further details see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 4.1</a>.</p><p>Absolute dating of the identified WGD event was performed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vanneste, K., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Tangled up in two: a burst of genome duplications at the end of the Cretaceous and the consequences for plant evolution. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. B Biol. Sci . 369, 20130353 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2245">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2248">29</a></sup>. In brief, paralogueous gene pairs located in duplicated segments (anchors) and duplicated pairs lying under the WGD peak (peak-based duplicates) were collected for phylogenetic dating. Anchors, assumed to be corresponding to the most recent WGD, were detected using i-ADHoRe 3.0 (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Proost, S. et al. i-ADHoRe 3.0-fast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e11 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2251">66</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Fostier, J. et al. A greedy, graph-based algorithm for the alignment of multiple homologous gene lists. Bioinformatics 27, 749–756 (2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2254">67</a>). Only a low number of duplicated segments and hence anchors could be identified, most likely because of the fragmented assembly of <i>Z. marina.</i> However, the identified anchors did confirm the presence of a broad WGD peak between a <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> of 0.8 and 1.6 (data not shown). For each WGD paralogueous pair, an orthogroup was created that included the two paralogues plus several orthologues from other plant species as identified by InParanoid (v. 4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Ostlund, G. et al. InParanoid 7: new algorithms and tools for eukaryotic orthology analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D196–D203 (2010)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2266">68</a></sup> using a broad taxonomic sampling: one representative orthologue from the order Cucurbitales, two from the Rosales, two from the Fabales, two from the Malpighiales, two from the Brassicales, one from the Malvales, one from the Solanales, two from the Poales, one orthologue from <i>Musa acuminata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2272">69</a></sup> (Zingiberales), and one orthologue from <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nature Commun . 5, 1–13 (2014)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2278">11</a></sup> (Alismatales). In total, about 180 orthogroups from anchor pair duplicates and peak-based duplicates were collected. The node joining the two <i>Z. marina</i> WGD paralogues was then dated using the BEAST v. 1.7 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Drummond, A. J., Suchard, M. A., Xie, D. &amp; Rambaut, A. 3408070; Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1969–1973 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2285">70</a></sup> under an uncorrelated relaxed clock model and a LG+G (four rate categories) evolutionary model. A starting tree with branch lengths satisfying all fossil prior constraints was created according to the consensus APGIII phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="The Angiosperm Phylogeny Group. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG III. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 161, 105–121 (2009)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2290">71</a></sup>. Fossil calibrations were implemented using log-normal calibration priors on the following nodes: the node uniting the Malvidae based on the fossil <i>Dressiantha bicarpellata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Gandolfo, M., Nixon, K. &amp; Crepet, W. A new fossil flower from the Turonian of New Jersey: Dressiantha bicarpellata gen. et sp. nov. (Capparales). Am. J. Bot. 85, 964–974 (1998)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2296">72</a></sup> with prior offset = 82.8, mean = 3.8528, and s.d. = 0.5)(ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Beilstein, M. A., Nagalingum, N. S., Clements, M. D., Manchester, S. R. &amp; Mathews, S. Dated molecular phylogenies indicate a Miocene origin for Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18724–18728 (2010)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2299">73</a>), the node uniting the Fabidae based on the fossil <i>Paleoclusia chevalieri</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Crepet, W. &amp; Nixon, K. C. Fossil Clusiaceae from the late Cretaceous (Turonian) of New Jersey and implications regarding the history of been pollination. Am. J. Bot. 85, 1122–1133 (1998)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2305">74</a></sup> with prior offset = 82.8, mean = 3.9314, and s.d. = 0.5(ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Xi, Z. et al. Phylogenomics and a posteriori data partitioning resolve the Cretaceous angiosperm radiation Malpighiales. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 17519–17524 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2308">75</a>), the node uniting the Alismatales (including <i>Z. marina</i> and <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>) with the other monocots based on the oldest fossil monocot pollen, <i>Liliacidites</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Doyle, J. A., Endress, P. K. &amp; Upchurch, G. R. Early Cretaceous monocots: a phylogenetic evaluation. Acta Musei Nationalis Pragae, Series B. Historia Naturalis 64, 59–87 (2008)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2321">76</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Iles, W. J. D., Smith, S. Y., Gandolfo, M. A. &amp; Graham, S. W. Monocot fossils suitable for molecular dating analyses. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 178, 346–374 (2015)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2324">77</a></sup> from the Trent’s Reach locality, with prior offset = 125, mean = 2.0418, and s.d. = 0.5 (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nauheimer, L., Metzler, D. &amp; Renner, S. S. Global history of the ancient monocot family Araceae inferred with models accounting for past continental positions and previous ranges based on fossils. New Phytol. 195, 938–950 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2327">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Janssen, T. &amp; Bremer, K. The age of major monocot groups inferred from 800+ rbcL sequences. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 146, 385–398 (2004)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2330">78</a>) and the root with prior offset = 124, mean = 4.0786, and s.d. = 0.5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Smith, S. A., Beaulieu, J. M. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. An uncorrelated relaxed-clock analysis suggests an earlier origin for flowering plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 5897–5902 (2010)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2334">79</a>). The offsets of these calibrations represent hard minimum boundaries, while their means represent locations for their respective peak mass probabilities in accordance with some of the most recent and taxonomically complete dating studies available for these specific clades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nauheimer, L., Metzler, D. &amp; Renner, S. S. Global history of the ancient monocot family Araceae inferred with models accounting for past continental positions and previous ranges based on fossils. New Phytol. 195, 938–950 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2338">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Clarke, J. T., Warnock, R. C. &amp; Donoghue, P. C. Establishing a time-scale for plant evolution. New Phytol. 192, 266–301 (2011)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2341">80</a></sup>. A run without data was performed to ensure proper placement of the marginal calibration prior distributions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Heled, J. &amp; Drummond, A. J. Calibrated tree priors for relaxed phylogenetics and divergence time estimation. Syst. Biol. 61, 138–149 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2345">81</a></sup>. The Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) for each orthogroup was run for 10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Mazzuca, S. et al. Establishing research strategies, methodologies and technologies to link genomics and proteomics to seagrass productivity, community metabolism, and ecosystem carbon fluxes. Front. Plant Sci. 4, 1–19 (2013)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2349">6</a></sup> generations, sampling every 1,000 generations resulting in a sample size of 10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Aquino, R. S., Landeira-Fernandez, A. M., Valente, A. P., Andrade, L. R. &amp; Mourao, P. A. S. Occurrence of sulfated galactans in marine angiosperms: evolutionary implications. Glycobiology 15, 11–20 (2005)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2353">4</a></sup>. The resulting trace files of all orthogroups were evaluated manually using Tracer v. 1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Drummond, A. J., Suchard, M. A., Xie, D. &amp; Rambaut, A. 3408070; Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1969–1973 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2357">70</a></sup> with a burn-in of 1,000 samples to ensure proper convergence (minimum ESS for all statistics at least 200). In total, 169 orthogroups were accepted and all age estimates for the node uniting the WGD paralogous pairs were then grouped into one absolute age distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>, too few anchors were available to evaluate them separately from the peak-based duplicates), for which kernel density estimation (KDE) and a bootstrapping procedure were used to find the peak consensus WGD age estimate and its 90% confidence interval boundaries, respectively.</p><p>Intra- and inter-genomic co-linearity was investigated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Tables 4.2 and 4.3</a>) using MCScan<span class="u-small-caps">X</span><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2373">82</a></sup> based on a BLASTP search of all genomic protein coding genes with an E-value cut-off of e<sup>−10</sup>. Only one large duplicated segment was detected, which was most likely due to the fragmented assembly of <i>Z. marina</i>; only 27 scaffolds had a size larger than 1 Mb, accounting for only 23.4% of all protein-coding genes. We therefore additionally used i-ADHoRe (v. 3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Proost, S. et al. i-ADHoRe 3.0-fast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e11 (2012)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2382">66</a></sup> to investigate genomic co-linearity by including all possible scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Gene family comparisons</h3><p>Protein sets were collected for 14 species: <i>Z. marina</i> (ORCAE v. 2.1), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (TAIR10), <i>Thellungiella parvula</i> (<a href="http://thellungiella.org">http://thellungiella.org</a>) <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (Phytozome v. 9.0), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Phytozome v. 9.0), <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> (<a href="http://amborella.huck.psu.edu">http://amborella.huck.psu.edu</a>), <i>Oryza sativa</i> japonica (Phytozome v. 9.0), <i>Zea mays</i> (Phytozome v. 9.0), <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (Phytozome v. 9.0), <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> (<a href="http://mocklerlab.org">http://mocklerlab.org</a>), <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> (Phytozome v. 9.0), <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> (Phytozome v. 9.0), <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> (Phytozome v. 9.0), and <i>Ostreococcus lucimarinus</i> (ORCAE v. 6/3/2013). These species were selected in order to provide a phylogenetic representation traversing green algae, basal plants, monocots, and dicots. Following an ‘all-vresus-all’ TimeLogic Decypher Tera-BLASTP (Active Motif Inc.; e-value threshold 1 × e<sup>−3</sup>, max hits 500) comparison, OrthoMCL (v. 2.0; mcl inflation factor 3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2462">83</a></sup> was used to delineate gene families. Confidence in establishing gene losses in <i>Zostera</i> was enhanced by using a combination of reciprocal blast, TblastN, re-annotation of <i>Spirodela</i> (and other monocot genes), and careful phylogenetic analysis. OrthoMCL results and related protein resources are available in the ORCAE download section.</p><p>To further understand gene family expansion or contraction in <i>Z. marina</i> in comparison with other sequenced genomes, gene family sizes were calculated for all gene families (excluding orphans and species-specific families) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 4.2</a>). The number of genes per species for each family was transformed into a matrix of <i>z</i>-scores in order to centre and normalize the data. The first 100 families with the largest gene family size in <i>Z. marina</i> were selected. The <i>z</i>-score profile was hierarchically clustered (complete linkage clustering) using Pearson correlation as a distance measure. The functional annotation of each family was predicted based on sequence similarity to entries in the InterProScan and Pfam protein domain database where more than 30% of proteins in the family share the same protein domain. The phylogenetic profile and phylogenetic tree topology provided at PLAZA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Proost, S. et al. PLAZA 3.0: an access point for plant comparative genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D974–D981 (2015)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2491">84</a></sup> were used to reconstruct the most parsimonious series of gene gain and loss events. The Dollop program from the PHYLIP package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Felsenstein, J. in PHYLIP: Phylogenetic inference program, version 3.6 (University of Washington, 2005)" href="/articles/nature16548#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d15965e2495">85</a></sup> was used to determine the minimum gene set at ancestral nodes of the phylogenetic tree. The Dollop program is based on the Dollo parsimony principle, which assumes that novel gene families arise exactly once during evolution but can be lost independently in different phylogenetic lineages.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Search for presence/absence of orthologues for specific genes and families</h3><p>A dedicated search for orthologues/homologues was performed for genes and proteins involved in stomata differentiation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 5.1</a>), volatile biosynthesis and sensing with focus on ethylene and terpenes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 6.1</a>), as well as genes involved in male flower specification and pollen differentiation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 11.1</a>). To this end, queries were chosen from documented genes involved in these pathways (usually from <i>Arabidopsis</i> but occasionally from <i>Oryza</i>, <i>Zea</i> and tomato). Next, the search for homologues in <i>Zostera marina</i>, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>, <i>Oryza sativa japonica</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (when not used as a query) was performed using <span class="u-small-caps">BLASTP</span>. To avoid missing or poorly annotated genes a <span class="u-small-caps">TBLASTN</span> search was conducted using the above queries against the <i>Zostera marina</i> and <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> genomes. Putative orthologues were identified based on reciprocal <span class="u-small-caps">BLASTP</span> searches with <i>Arabidopsis</i> (or the other queries). Owing to species-specific duplications, this may produce some paralogous genes to appear orthologous to the query, or vice versa (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab1">Extended Data Tables 1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab2">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#Tab3">3</a>). To further confirm correct orthology assignments, phylogenetic trees were built using a broader sampling of protein sequences from both the query species and the three target species. Ambiguously aligned sequences (especially due to indels) were checked manually and corrected or removed.</p></div></div></section>

Further information on the <i>Zostera marina</i> project is available via the Online Resource for Community Annotation Eukaryotes (ORCA) at <a href="http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/orcae/">http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/orcae/</a>.</p>

                        <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Yves+Van de Peer" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide"> </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Yves+Van de Peer%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>J.L.O., T.B.H.R., G.P. and Y.V.d.P. are the lead investigators and contributed equally to the work. J.S., J.W.J., J.G., Y.V.d.P., B.V. and Y.-C.L. coordinated the bioinformatics activities surrounding assembly, quality control, set-up and maintenance of <i>Z. marina</i> on the ORCAE site and deposition of the <i>Z. marina</i> genome resource. T.B.H.R and T.B. generated and analysed RNA-seq libraries from flowers, rhizome, roots. J.L.O., Y.-C.L. and A.J. generated and analysed RNA-seq libraries from the genome genotype and temperature stress experiments. C.B., W.T.S. and J.L.O. contributed to biological sample collection, preparation and quality control prior to DNA extraction. A.M. performed the HMW DNA extraction and quality control from the genome genotype/clone. M.A., J.G., H.T. and M.C. contributed to WGS libraries and sequencing, (fosmid)-cloning and quality control. J.G. coordinated the sequencing of FES, quality control projects. Analysis of architectural features of the genome and annotation of specific gene families, including the written contributions to the main paper and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Information</a> sections, were performed by the following co-authors: J.W.J., the chromosome assembly analysis; B.V. and Y.-C.L., gene family clustering and comparative phylogenomics; A.R.K. and E.B.B., Pfam domains; E.D.P. and P.J.G., miRNA; R.L., K.V. and Y.V.d.P., whole-genome duplication; F.M., Y.-C.L. and Y.V.d.P., transposable elements; B.V., co-linearity and synteny comparisons; M.T., organellar genomes; P.R., stomata gene family; G.M., cell wall polysaccharides and sulfotransferases; T.T., fatty acid metabolism and its relationship to cell walls and ion homeostasis; P.R., volatiles (ethylene, terpenes); P.R., J.B. and T.B.H.R., metallothioniens; P.R., G.A.P. and C.L., osmoregulation/ion homeostasis/stress-related genes; S.D. and E.D., photosynthetic/ light-sensing genes; G.M., CAZymes; T.B., T.B.H.R. and P.R., plant defence-related; T.B. assembly and analysis of MADS box genes (flowering); P.R.; Y.V.d.P. and Y.-C.L., pollen-related and self-incompatibility genes; F.M., SLR-1gene and core eukaryotic genes analysis (CEGMA). J.L.O., Y.V.d.P., T.B.H.R., C.M.D., Y.-C.L. and P.R. wrote and edited the main manuscript (including the Methods and Extended Data), and organized and further edited the individual contributions (as listed above) for the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Information</a> sections. J.L.O. and Y.V.d.P. provided the overall evolutionary context and T.B.H.R., G.P. and C.M.D. provided the ecological and societal context. All authors read and commented on the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature16548/email/correspondent/c1/new">Jeanine L. Olsen</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature16548/email/correspondent/c2/new">Yves Van de Peer</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">

            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Extended data figures and tables"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Extended data figures and tables</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig4"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/4" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig4_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 1 Number of genes expressed in five tissues of <i>Z. marina</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Venn diagram of genes with expression values (FPKM) higher than 1 are considered as expressed in the tissue. <b>b</b>, Pairwise differential gene expression analysis between tissues. The male flower shows the highest number of differentially expressed genes.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig5"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/5" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig5_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 2 Circos plot of the ten largest scaffolds of <i>Z. marina</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Tracks from outside to inside. GC percentage, gene density, and transposable element (TE) density (density measured in 20-Kb sliding windows and gene expression profiles from five tissues (root, leaf, male flower, female flower early and female flower late) presented as log<sub>2</sub> FPKM values.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig6"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/6" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig6_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 3 Potential impact of transposable elements (TEs) on <i>Z. marina</i> evolution.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Frequency distribution of pairwise sequence identity values between copies of Copia- and Gypsy-type LTR retrotransposons and DNA transposons, and their cognate consensus sequences (younger repeats share higher sequence similarity). Two peaks are detectable for Copia-type elements. <b>b</b>, Distance to the closest TE for the set of <i>Z. marina</i> single-copy genes and the set of <i>Z. marina</i> accessory genes. TE-proximal accessory genes are more frequent than TE-proximal single-copy genes. <b>c</b>, Frequency of pairwise sequence identity between accessory gene-proximal Ty3-Gypsy elements and their cognate consensus sequences. A number of high-identity copies (that is, putatively young duplicate genes) is observed.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 4 Unrooted maximum likelihood tree of genes encoding light-harvesting complex A (LHCA) and LHCB proteins of <i>Z.marina</i>, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The analysis was carried out on protein sequences using PhyML 3 with LG substitution model and 100 bootstrap replicates. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 7.1, Supplementary Table 7.3</a>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 5 Alignment of metallothionein (MT) and half-metallothionein (HMT) genes in <i>Z. marina</i> as compared with other plants.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Alignments were performed in ClustalW on the Lyon PBIL web server and edited manually. The upper alignments are for type 1–3 MTs and HMTs; the lower alignment is for type 4 EcMTs where there is no <i>Zostera</i> homologue. Conserved residues are shown in red and residues in the same amino acid group in blue. Cys and His residues, putatively involved in binding metals, are highlighted in green and yellow, respectively. Aromatic amino acids absent in canonical animal MTs are highlighted in grey. MTs and MT-like proteins were obtained from: <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (ARATH), Japanese rice (ORYSJ), <i>Cicer arietinum</i> (CICAR), banana (MUSAC), wheat (WHEAT), potato (SOLTU), <i>Setaria Italica</i> (SETIT), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (VITVI) and the alismatids: <i>Posidonia oceanica</i> (POSOC) highlighted in grey, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> (SPIPO) highlighted in blue, and <i>Zostera marina</i> (ZOSMA) highlighted in yellow. See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature16548#MOESM19">Supplementary Note 8.2</a>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature16548/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature16548/MediaObjects/41586_2016_Article_BFnature16548_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 6 Conceptual summary of physiological and structural adaptations made by <i>Z. marina</i> in its return to the sea.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Ecosystem services shown in blue. Physical processes related to salinity, light and CO<sub>2</sub> availability shown in white within light-green boxes. Gene losses and gains associated with morphological and physiological processes shown in white within the dark-green box on the right.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item"                     class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
                    id="authors-3">

            </li>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">The genome of the seagrass <i>Zostera marina</i></h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>The seagrass <i>Zostera marina</i>, or eelgrass, is widely distributed throughout the Northern Hemisphere. It is therefore of considerable ecological importance but — as with other seagrasses — its coastal habitats are among the world's most threatened ecosystems. Jeanine Olsen and colleagues report the whole-genome sequence of <i>Zostera</i>. Their analyses provide insights into the evolutionary changes associated with the 'back to the sea' reverse evolutionary trajectory that has occurred in this angiosperm lineage, including the loss of the entire repertoire of stomatal genes, and the presence of sulfated cell-wall polysaccharides that are more macro-algal-like than plant-like.</p></div></section></div></aside>
